<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passaporte_portugu%C3%AAs_de_1927.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Passaporte_portugu%C3%AAs_de_1927.jpg/220px-Passaporte_portugu%C3%AAs_de_1927.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Passaporte_portugu%C3%AAs_de_1927.jpg/330px-Passaporte_portugu%C3%AAs_de_1927.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Passaporte_portugu%C3%AAs_de_1927.jpg/440px-Passaporte_portugu%C3%AAs_de_1927.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1799" data-file-height="1318" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passaporte_portugu%C3%AAs_de_1927.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Passport of an immigrant from the <a href="/wiki/Braga_District" title="Braga District">Braga District</a> to Brazil</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Brazil" title="Colonial Brazil">colonial times</a>, over 700,000 Portuguese settled in Brazil, and most of them went there during the gold rush of the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
Brazil received more European settlers during its colonial era than any other country in the Americas. Between 1500 and 1760, about 700,000 Europeans immigrated to Brazil, compared to 530,000 European immigrants in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Pinto_Venâncio_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinto_Venâncio-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
They managed to be the only significant European population to populate the country during <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a>, even though there were French and Dutch invasions. The Portuguese migration was strongly marked by the predominance of <a href="/wiki/Men" class="mw-redirect" title="Men">men</a> (colonial reports from the 16th and 17th centuries almost always report the absence or rarity of Portuguese <a href="/wiki/Women" class="mw-redirect" title="Women">women</a>). This lack of women worried the Jesuits, who asked the Portuguese King to send any kind of Portuguese women to Brazil, even the socially undesirable (e.g. prostitutes or women with mental maladies such as Down Syndrome) if necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-ensinarhistoria_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ensinarhistoria-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The Crown responded by sending groups of Iberian orphan maidens to marry both cohorts of marriageable men, the nobles and the peasants. Some of which were even primarily studying to be nuns.<sup id="cite_ref-ensinarhistoria_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ensinarhistoria-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The Crown also shipped over many <a href="/wiki/%C3%93rf%C3%A3s_d%27El-Rei" title="Órfãs d&#39;El-Rei">Órfãs d'El-Rei</a> of what was considered "good birth" to <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Brazil" title="Colonial Brazil">colonial Brazil</a> to marry Portuguese settlers of high rank. <i>Órfãs d'El-Rei</i> (modern Portuguese <i>órfãs do rei</i>) literally translates to "Orphans of the King", and they were Portuguese female orphans in nubile age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETelfer1932184_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETelfer1932184-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> There were noble and non-noble maidens and they were daughters of military compatriots who died in battle for the king or noblemen who died overseas and whose upbringing was paid by the Crown.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETelfer1932184_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETelfer1932184-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Bahia's port in the East received one of the first groups of orphans in 1551.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethell198447_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethell198447-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The multiplication of <a href="/wiki/Lineal_descendant" title="Lineal descendant">descendants</a> of Portuguese settlers also happened to a large degree through <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a> with <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">black</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amerindian" class="mw-redirect" title="Amerindian">amerindian</a> women. In fact, in colonial Brazil the Portuguese men competed for the women, because among the <a href="/wiki/Afro-Brazilians" title="Afro-Brazilians">African slaves</a> the female component was also a small minority.<sup id="cite_ref-Ribeiro_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ribeiro-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> This explains why the Portuguese men left more descendants in Brazil than the Amerindian or African men did. The Indian and African women were "dominated" by the Portuguese men, preventing men of color to find partners with whom they could have children. Added to this, White people had a much better <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a> and therefore a lower <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rate</a> than the black and indigenous population. Then, even though the Portuguese migration during colonial Brazil was smaller (3.2 million Indians estimated at the beginning of colonization and 3.6 million Africans brought since then, compared to the descendants of the over 700,000 Portuguese immigrants) the "white" population (whose ancestry was predominantly Portuguese) was as large as the "non white" population in the early 19th century, just before independence from Portugal.<sup id="cite_ref-Ribeiro_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ribeiro-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
After <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Brazil" title="Independence of Brazil">independence from Portugal</a> in 1822, around 1.7 million Portuguese immigrants settled in Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-Ribeiro_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ribeiro-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
Portuguese immigration into Brazil in the 19th and 20th centuries was marked by its concentration in the states of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">São Paulo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro_(state)" title="Rio de Janeiro (state)">Rio de Janeiro</a>. The immigrants opted mostly for <a href="/wiki/Urban_center" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban center">urban centers</a>. Portuguese women appeared with some regularity among immigrants, with percentage variation in different decades and regions of the country. However, even among the more recent influx of Portuguese immigrants at the turn of the 20th century, there were 319 men to each 100 women among them.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The Portuguese were different from other immigrants in Brazil, like the Germans,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> or Italians<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> who brought many women along with them (even though the proportion of men was higher in any immigrant community). Despite the small female proportion, Portuguese men married mainly Portuguese women. Female immigrants rarely married Brazilian men. In this context, the Portuguese had a rate of <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">endogamy</a> which was higher than any other European immigrant community, and behind only the Japanese among all immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso_(1999).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso_%281999%29.jpg/180px-Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso_%281999%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso_%281999%29.jpg/270px-Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso_%281999%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso_%281999%29.jpg/360px-Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso_%281999%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="736" data-file-height="1009" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso_(1999).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Former Brazilian President <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Henrique_Cardoso" title="Fernando Henrique Cardoso">Fernando Henrique Cardoso</a> descends from Portuguese immigrants</div></div></div>
<p>Even with Portuguese heritage, many Portuguese-Brazilians identify themselves as being simply Brazilians, since Portuguese culture was a dominant cultural influence in the formation of Brazil (like many <a href="/wiki/British_Americans" title="British Americans">British Americans</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, who will never describe themselves as of British extraction, but only as "Americans", since British  culture was a dominant cultural influence in the formation of The United States).
</p><p>In 1872, there were 3.7 million <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">Whites</a> in Brazil (the vast majority of them of Portuguese ancestry), 4.1 million <a href="/wiki/Mixed-race" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed-race">mixed-race</a> people (mostly of Portuguese-<a href="/wiki/Afro-Brazilians" title="Afro-Brazilians">African</a>-<a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Brazil" title="Indigenous peoples in Brazil">Amerindian</a> ancestry) and 1.9 million <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">Blacks</a>. These numbers give the percentage of 80% of people with total or partial Portuguese ancestry in Brazil in the 1870s.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 19th and early 20th centuries, a new large wave of immigrants from Portugal arrived. From 1881 to 1991, over 1.5 million Portuguese immigrated to Brazil. In 1906, for example, there were 133,393 Portuguese-born people living in <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, comprising 16% of the city's population. Rio is, still today, considered the largest "Portuguese city" outside of Portugal itself, with 1% Portuguese-born people.<sup id="cite_ref-Pinto_Venâncio_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinto_Venâncio-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Genetic studies also confirm the strong Portuguese genetic influence in Brazilians. According to a study, at least half of the Brazilian population's <a href="/wiki/Y_Chromosome" class="mw-redirect" title="Y Chromosome">Y Chromosome</a> (male inheritance) comes from Portugal. <a href="/wiki/Afro-Brazilians" title="Afro-Brazilians">Black Brazilians</a> have an average of 48% non-African genes, most of them may come from Portuguese ancestors. On the other hand, 33% Amerindian and 28% African contribution to the total mtDNA (female inheritance) of white Brazilians was found<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PLoS_ONE_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PLoS_ONE-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Autosome" title="Autosome">autosomal</a> study from 2013, with nearly 1300 samples from all of the Brazilian regions, found a predominant degree of European ancestry (mostly Portuguese, due to the dominant Portuguese influx among European colonization and immigration to Brazil) combined with African and Native American contributions, in varying degrees. 'Following an increasing North to South gradient, European ancestry was the most prevalent in all urban populations (with values from 51% to 74%). The populations in the North consisted of a significant proportion of Native American ancestry that was about two times higher than the African contribution. Conversely, in the Northeast, Center-West and Southeast, African ancestry was the second most prevalent. At an intrapopulation level, all urban
populations were highly admixed, and most of the variation in ancestry proportions was observed between individuals within each population rather than among population'.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A large community-based multicenter autosomal study from 2015, considering representative samples from three different urban communities located in the Northeast (<a href="/wiki/Salvador,_Bahia" title="Salvador, Bahia">Salvador</a>, capital of <a href="/wiki/Bahia" title="Bahia">Bahia</a>), Southeast (<a href="/wiki/Bambu%C3%AD" title="Bambuí">Bambuí</a>, interior of Minas Gerais) and South Brazilian (<a href="/wiki/Pelotas" title="Pelotas">Pelotas</a>, interior of Rio Grande do Sul) regions, estimated European ancestry to be 42.4%, 83.8% and 85.3%, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In all three cities, European ancestors were mainly Iberian.
</p><p>It was estimated that around 5 million Brazilians (2,5% of the population) can acquire Portuguese citizenship, due to the last <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_nationality_law" title="Portuguese nationality law">Portuguese nationality law</a> that grants citizenship to grandchildren of Portuguese nationals.<sup id="cite_ref-noticiaslusofonas.com_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noticiaslusofonas.com-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:R1b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="R1b.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/R1b.jpg/220px-R1b.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/R1b.jpg/330px-R1b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/R1b.jpg/440px-R1b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="555" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:R1b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>According to an early genetic study, the Portuguese are a relatively distinct population according to HLA data, as they have a high frequency of the HLA-A25-B18-DR15 and A26-B38-DR13 genes. The latter is a unique Portuguese marker — the Portuguese have neither a significant contribution to their genetic pool from paleo-North Africans (A30-B18) nor Mediterraneans (A33-B14). As such, they may have remained in relative genetic isolation compared to the rest of the Iberian populations. In Europe, the A25-B18-DR15 gene is only found in Portugal, and is also observed in white North Americans and in Brazilians (very likely of Portuguese ancestry).<sup id="cite_ref-portgenes_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-portgenes-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pan-European (most probably <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a>) haplotype <a href="/wiki/HLA_A1-B8-DR3-DQ2" title="HLA A1-B8-DR3-DQ2">A1-B8-DR3</a> and the western-European haplotype A29-B44-DR7 are shared by Portuguese, <a href="/wiki/Basques" title="Basques">Basques</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spaniards" title="Spaniards">Spaniards</a>. The later is also common in <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>, and western <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> populations.<sup id="cite_ref-portgenes_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-portgenes-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The A2-B7-DR15 gene is common to those, the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_people" title="Cornish people">Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austrians" title="Austrians">Austrians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">Germans</a>, showing a much more ancient link between <a href="/wiki/North_Africans" class="mw-redirect" title="North Africans">North Africans</a> and western and central <a href="/wiki/Europeans" class="mw-redirect" title="Europeans">Europeans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-portgenes_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-portgenes-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>R1b-5 gene cluster is a male re-expansion 15,000-13,000 years ago from Northwestern <a href="/wiki/Iberia" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberia">Iberia</a> heading towards <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and Northern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.  The Rory gene cluster (R1b-14) is one of the largest re-expansions also head towards Ireland and Scotland, however featuring particularly in Irish men with Gaelic names.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Portuguese ethnicity is more clear-cut than Spanish ethnicity, but here also, the case is complicated by the Portuguese ancestry of populations in the former <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">colonial empire</a>. Portugal has 10 million nationals. The 40 million figure is due to a study estimating a total of an additional 31 million  descendants from Portuguese including grandparents; these people would be eligible for Portuguese citizenship under <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_nationality_law" title="Portuguese nationality law">Portuguese nationality law</a> (which  grants citizenship to grandchildren of Portuguese nationals). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.dn.pt/inicio/portugal/interior.aspx?content_id=1192698"><i>Emigração: A diáspora dos portugueses</i></a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20141028023159/http://www.dn.pt/inicio/portugal/interior.aspx?content_id=1192698">Archived</a> 28 October 2014 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> (2009)</span>
